الاسم المشترك كما يقال صوت صاف ويقال صوت كدر، فهنا لا ينتفع فيه بذلك القانون. ولا يوجد من جهة التسمية مخلص غير الشبهة بل الحد والماهية والعلامة والخاصة هي التي تدل في أمثالها على الاشتراك. فإن الصفاء لو كان له فيهما معنًى واحد لكان مدركة حاسة واحدة لكن ليس كذلك بل يدرك أحدهما السمع، والآخر البصر. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور، فهذه قوانين من جهة التضاد؛ فإن لم يكن للشيء مقابل بل بحسب التضاد يكون له مقابل على حسب التناقض البسيط لا محالة. وعرفت الفرق بينه وبين التناقض فإن وجدت اللفظة السلبية مشتركة فكذلك اللفظة الإيجابية كقولنا لا بصير ؛ ولا يستعمل البصير. فإن أحدهما إذا كان مشتركًا فالبصير بإزائه مشترك فيه. وكذلك من طريق العلم والعدم فإنه إن كان البصر على وجهين؛ بصر قلب وبصر عين فكذلك العمى. وأيضًا يجب أن يعتبر التصاريف في الاشتقاقات؛ فإن العدالة إذا كانت مشتركةً فيها كان العدل مشتركًا، وإن كان المُصح مشتركا كان الصحيح مشتركًا. وأيضًا أن ترفع الأمور المسماة بالأسماء إلى أجناسها؛ فإن اختلف ارتفاعها كان الاسم مشتركًا فإن الخير إذا قيل للملك ، وقيل للفضيلة، وقيل للمساوي. وحد الأول يرتقي إلى الجوهر، والثاني إلى الكم، والثالث إلى الكم؛ فيكون اسم الخير إذن يقع عليها بمعان مختلفة. اللهم إلا أن يُعنى بالخير أمر من الأمور اللازمة حتى يكون من الأسماء المشكة إلا أن الخير ليس كذلك. فإن الملك خير على أنه جوهر كامل الوجود ، وليس فيه ما بالقوة. وكذلك إن لم يرتفع إلى أجناس عالية مختلفة بل أجناس متوسطة مختلفة مثل الأبيض في الألوان والأبيض في الأصوات، ومثل الحاد من الأصوات والحاد من الزاوية؛ فإنها ليست ترتفع إلى أجناس عالية بل ترتفع إلى أجناس قريبة مختلفة. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور فإنه قد يعرض من جهة الأجناس والأنواع التي بعضها فوق بعض أن يكون الواحد بالاشتراك على شيء واحد من حيث يدل على الأعم والأخص. وأيضًا يجب أن تنظر في فصول ما يدل عليه بالاسم هل هي مختلفة أو هل هي فصول واحدة بأعيانها؛ فإن وجدت الفصول المختلفة يكون الاسم مشتركًا فإنك تجد فصول اللون الذي يقال على البصير مفرَّقًا للبصير وجامعا له، وفصول اللون الذي يدل على أحد الأجناس الثلاثة التي في الألحان جما وبدنا فإلا كانت الفصول مختلفة كان اللون اسما.
But that he should have the ability to present the limits of what is shared in the limits that indicate the distinction of some from others: so we do not say, for example, that 'good' is said of courage, justice, chastity, and the [؟] and the [؟] in only one meaning, but rather he should clarify how that is, so he explains, for example, that courage and justice indicate that they are a quality of the good, and the [؟] and the [؟] as an agent of the quality. As for the useful laws for knowing that a name that applies to many things and they are essences with different quiddities and realities, or it applies to them with one meaning: among them is that if the thing has an opposite, then one looks: are the enumerated things opposite to the things that agree in that name one in name or different? If he finds them different in name at the outset, it becomes easy for him to determine that the name is a shared name; and if he does not find them different in names, then it is difficult for him. For it is not impossible that there be a shared name and opposite to it a shared name, like its opposite. An example of the first is that the name 'sharp' applies to the sword and to sound. It has, in terms of its application to sound, a specific opposite named 'dull [in sound]', and in terms of its application to the sword, a specific opposite named 'blunt'. He finds them different; thus 'sharp' in the sword and sound is by sharing of the name. And so on in other cases. As for the second example, it is that the opposite is one in the shared name, as it is said 'clear sound' and it is said 'muddy sound'; here this law is not beneficial. And there is no deliverance from the aspect of naming except by ambiguity, but rather the definition, quiddity, mark, and property are what indicate in such cases the sharing. For if clarity had one meaning in both, they would be perceived by one sense, but it is not so; rather, one is perceived by hearing, the other by sight. And so on in other cases. These are laws from the aspect of opposition. If the thing has no opposite, but rather by way of opposition it has an opposite according to simple contradiction necessarily. And you know the difference between it and contradiction. If you find the negative expression shared, then similarly the positive expression, like our saying 'non-seeing' and 'seeing' [؟]. For if one of them is shared, then 'seeing' opposite to it is shared in it. And likewise by way of knowledge and privation: if 'sight' is of two kinds – sight of the heart and sight of the eye – then similarly 'blindness'. Also one must consider the conjugations in derivations: if 'justice' is shared in them, then 'just' is shared; and if 'sickness' is shared, then 'sick' is shared. Also, one should elevate the things named by the names to their genera: if their elevation differs, then the name is shared. For 'good' is said of the king, of virtue, and of the mean. The definition of the first elevates to substance, the second to quality, the third to quality; thus the name 'good' then applies to them with different meanings. Unless by 'good' is meant an essential matter such that it would be among the shared names – but 'good' is not like that. For the king is good as a substance complete in existence, having no potentiality. Likewise if they do not elevate to different highest genera but to different intermediate genera, like 'white' in colors and 'white' in sounds, and like 'sharp' in sounds and 'sharp' in angles; they do not elevate to highest genera but to different proximate genera. And so on in other cases. For it may occur from the aspect of genera and species, some above others, that one thing is by sharing in that it indicates the more general and the more specific. Also, one must look at the differentiate of what is indicated by the name: are they different or are they the same differentiate? If you find the differentiate different, then the name is shared. For you find the differentiate of color that is said of the seeing – separating and gathering the seeing – and the differentiate of color that indicates one of the three genera in melodies [؟] – if the differentiate were different, then color is a name.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents a series of logical and linguistic laws (qawanin) for determining whether a name applied to multiple things is shared (mushtarak, i.e., homonymous) or univocal. The author begins by emphasizing that one must not only say that a term like 'good' applies in multiple meanings, but must also explain how those meanings differ (e.g., as quality vs. agent). Then he lists several tests. First, the law of opposites: if the things named have different opposites, the name is likely shared. Example: 'sharp' (had) applied to sword and sound has opposite 'blunt' (kalil) for sword and 'dull' (naql?) for sound, proving sharing. However, if the opposite is the same (e.g., 'clear' and 'muddy' for sound), this test fails, and one must rely on definition, essence, or property. Second, if a name has no direct opposite, one uses the opposite of its negation: if the negative expression is shared, the positive is also shared. Third, consider privation: if sight (basar) has two types (heart and eye), then blindness also has two types, indicating sharing. Fourth, check derivations: if a root noun is shared, its derived forms (e.g., 'justice' -> 'just') are shared. Fifth, elevate the named things to their genera: if they elevate to different highest genera (e.g., substance vs. quality), the name is shared. Example: 'good' said of king (substance), virtue (quality), and the mean (quality) – though the last two share a genus. If they elevate to different intermediate genera (e.g., 'white' in color vs. sound), that also indicates sharing. Sixth, examine the differentiate: if the differentiate of the named things differ, the name is shared. Example: 'color' applied to visible color and musical color has different differentiate. Each law provides a methodical way to resolve ambiguity in terms, crucial for logical analysis and definition.

B) It has two different opposites: 'blunt' for sword and 'dull' for sound.
C) It has no opposite at all.
D) Its negative is shared.

Key Takeaway
The law of opposites provides a quick way to detect shared names: if the things named have different opposites, the name is likely shared.

Laws of Privation, Derivation, and Genera
Author explains three additional laws for detecting shared names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the laws of privation, derivation, and genus elevation to detect shared names in logical texts.

Core Idea
When the opposite test fails, privations, derivations, and genera provide alternative methods to identify shared names.

The author continues with three additional tests: privation (if the positive term has multiple types, the privative also has multiple types), derivation (if a root noun is shared, derived forms are shared), and elevation to genera (if the things named lift to different highest genera, the name is shared). These tests supplement the law of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Examine privation (al-ʿadam wa al-malaka)
If the positive term (e.g., 'sight') has multiple types (e.g., heart's sight and eye's sight), then the privative term (e.g., 'blindness') also has multiple types, indicating the name is shared.
This is based on the pairing of possession and privation.
2
Check derivations (al-taṣārīf fī al-ishtiqāq)
If a root noun is shared (e.g., 'justice' in multiple senses), then its derivatives (e.g., 'just') are also shared.
This test leverages morphological relationships.
3
Elevate to genera (al-jins)
Take the things named and see what highest genus each belongs to. If they belong to different highest genera (e.g., substance vs. quality), the name is shared.
Even if they belong to different intermediate genera (e.g., 'white' in color vs. sound), that also indicates sharing.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the name 'khayr' (good) applied to a king (substance), virtue (quality), and the mean (quality). The first elevates to substance, the next two to quality. Since the genera differ, 'khayr' is shared.
This illustrates the genus test from the passage.

بل أن تكون له قدرة على إيراد حدود ما يشترك فيه للحدود التي تدل على مباينة بعضها البعض : فلا نقول مثلا إن الخير لا يقال على الشجاعة والعدل والعفة والمُصح والمُحصن بمعنى واحد فقط بل وأن تبين كيفية ذلك فتبين مثلا أن الشجاعة والعدل يشير على أنها كيفية للخير، والمُصح والمُحصن على أنه فاعل للكيفية. وأما القوانين النافعة في معرفة أن الاسم الواقع على أشياء كثيرة تقع عليها وهي ذوات ماهيات وحقائق مختلفة أو تقع عليها بمعنى واحد؛ فمنها إن كان للشيء ضد فينظر هل الأشياء المعدودة مضادة للأشياء المتفقة في ذلك الاسم هي واحدة في الاسم أو مختلفة: فإن وجدها مختلفة الاسم في أول الأمر سهل عليه الوقوف على أن الاسم اسم مشترك، وإن لم يجدها مختلفة الأسماء فقد تعذر عليه. فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون اسم مشترك وبإزائه اسم مشترك كالمضاد له، مثال الأول أن الاسم الحاد يقع على السيف وعلى الصوت. ويوجد له من حيث يقع على الصوت مضاد مخصوص باسم النقل، ومن حيث يقع على السيف مضاد مخصوص باسم الكليل وجداهما مختلفان؛ فيكون الحاد في السيف والصوت باشتراك الاسم، وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور. وأما مثال الثاني فهو أن يكون المضاد واحدي الاسم المشترك كما يقال صوت صاف ويقال صوت كدر، فهنا لا ينتفع فيه بذلك القانون. ولا يوجد من جهة التسمية مخلص غير الشبهة بل الحد والماهية والعلامة والخاصة هي التي تدل في أمثالها على الاشتراك. فإن الصفاء لو كان له فيهما معنًى واحد لكان مدركة حاسة واحدة لكن ليس كذلك بل يدرك أحدهما السمع، والآخر البصر. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور، فهذه قوانين من جهة التضاد؛ فإن لم يكن للشيء مقابل بل بحسب التضاد يكون له مقابل على حسب التناقض البسيط لا محالة. وعرفت الفرق بينه وبين التناقض فإن وجدت اللفظة السلبية مشتركة فكذلك اللفظة الإيجابية كقولنا لا بصير ؛ ولا يستعمل البصير. فإن أحدهما إذا كان مشتركًا فالبصير بإزائه مشترك فيه. وكذلك من طريق العلم والعدم فإنه إن كان البصر على وجهين؛ بصر قلب وبصر عين فكذلك العمى. وأيضًا يجب أن يعتبر التصاريف في الاشتقاقات؛ فإن العدالة إذا كانت مشتركةً فيها كان العدل مشتركًا، وإن كان المُصح مشتركا كان الصحيح مشتركًا. وأيضًا أن ترفع الأمور المسماة بالأسماء إلى أجناسها؛ فإن اختلف ارتفاعها كان الاسم مشتركًا فإن الخير إذا قيل للملك ، وقيل للفضيلة، وقيل للمساوي. وحد الأول يرتقي إلى الجوهر، والثاني إلى الكم، والثالث إلى الكم؛ فيكون اسم الخير إذن يقع عليها بمعان مختلفة. اللهم إلا أن يُعنى بالخير أمر من الأمور اللازمة حتى يكون من الأسماء المشكة إلا أن الخير ليس كذلك. فإن الملك خير على أنه جوهر كامل الوجود ، وليس فيه ما بالقوة. وكذلك إن لم يرتفع إلى أجناس عالية مختلفة بل أجناس متوسطة مختلفة مثل الأبيض في الألوان والأبيض في الأصوات، ومثل الحاد من الأصوات والحاد من الزاوية؛ فإنها The Law of Differentiae
Author concludes with the law of differentiae as a criterion for shared names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the law of differentiae to determine whether a name is shared by comparing the differentiae of its applications.

Core Idea
If the differentiae (fuṣūl) of the things named by a word are different, the word is a shared name.

The final law in this passage examines the differentiae (the distinguishing features) of the things named. If the differentiate that define each application are different, the name is shared. This method is especially useful when other tests are ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the name and its applications
Take a word that applies to multiple things. For each thing, determine its differentia (the feature that distinguishes it within its genus).
Differentiae are often part of the definition (ḥadd).
2
Compare the differentiae
If the differentiae are different sets, then the name is shared. If they are the same, the name may be univocal.
The author uses the example of 'color' (lawn) applied to visible color and to musical color.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the name 'ḥukm' (ruling) used in usul al-fiqh for a legal ruling and in logic for a proposition. The differentia of a legal ruling is 'taʿlīq al-ḥukm bi-ʿilla' (attachment of ruling to a cause), while the differentia of a logical proposition is 'iḥtimāl al-ṣidq wa al-kidhb' (susceptibility to truth and falsehood). Since the differentiae differ, 'ḥukm' is a shared name.
This example shows the test's relevance across disciplines.

الاسم المشترك كما يقال صوت صاف ويقال صوت كدر، فهنا لا ينتفع فيه بذلك القانون. ولا يوجد من جهة التسمية مخلص غير الشبهة بل الحد والماهية والعلامة والخاصة هي التي تدل في أمثالها على الاشتراك. فإن الصفاء لو كان له فيهما معنًى واحد لكان مدركة حاسة واحدة لكن ليس كذلك بل يدرك أحدهما السمع، والآخر البصر. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور، فهذه قوانين من جهة التضاد؛ فإن لم يكن للشيء مقابل بل بحسب التضاد يكون له مقابل على حسب التناقض البسيط لا محالة. وعرفت الفرق بينه وبين التناقض فإن وجدت اللفظة السلبية مشتركة فكذلك اللفظة الإيجابية كقولنا لا بصير ؛ ولا يستعمل البصير. فإن أحدهما إذا كان مشتركًا فالبصير بإزائه مشترك فيه. وكذلك من طريق العلم والعدم فإنه إن كان البصر على وجهين؛ بصر قلب وبصر عين فكذلك العمى. وأيضًا يجب أن يعتبر التصاريف في الاشتقاقات؛ فإن العدالة إذا كانت مشتركةً فيها كان العدل مشتركًا، وإن كان المُصح مشتركا كان الصحيح مشتركًا. وأيضًا أن ترفع الأمور المسماة بالأسماء إلى أجناسها؛ فإن اختلف ارتفاعها كان الاسم مشتركًا فإن الخير إذا قيل للملك ، وقيل للفضيلة، وقيل للمساوي. وحد الأول يرتقي إلى الجوهر، والثاني إلى الكم، والثالث إلى الكم؛ فيكون اسم الخير إذن يقع عليها بمعان مختلفة. اللهم إلا أن يُعنى بالخير أمر من الأمور اللازمة حتى يكون من الأسماء المشكة إلا أن الخير ليس كذلك. فإن الملك خير على أنه جوهر كامل الوجود ، وليس فيه ما بالقوة. وكذلك إن لم يرتفع إلى أجناس عالية مختلفة بل أجناس متوسطة مختلفة مثل الأبيض في الألوان والأبيض في الأصوات، ومثل الحاد من الأصوات والحاد من الزاوية؛ فإنها ليست ترتفع إلى أجناس عالية بل ترتفع إلى أجناس قريبة مختلفة. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور فإنه قد يعرض من جهة الأجناس والأنواع التي بعضها فوق بعض أن يكون الواحد بالاشتراك على شيء واحد من حيث يدل على الأعم والأخص. وأيضًا يجب أن تنظر في فصول ما يدل عليه بالاسم هل هي مختلفة أو هل هي فصول واحدة بأعيانها؛ فإن وجدت الفصول المختلفة يكون الاسم مشتركًا فإنك تجد فصول اللون الذي يقال على البصير مفرَّقًا للبصير وجامعا له، وفصول اللون الذي يدل على أحد الأجناس الثلاثة التي في الألحان جما وبدنا فإلا كانت الفصول مختلفة كان اللون اسما.
But that he should have the ability to present the limits of what is shared in the limits that indicate the distinction of some from others: so we do not say, for example, that 'good' is said of courage, justice, chastity, and the [؟] and the [؟] in only one meaning, but rather he should clarify how that is, so he explains, for example, that courage and justice indicate that they are a quality of the good, and the [؟] and the [؟] as an agent of the quality. As for the useful laws for knowing that a name that applies to many things and they are essences with different quiddities and realities, or it applies to them with one meaning: among them is that if the thing has an opposite, then one looks: are the enumerated things opposite to the things that agree in that name one in name or different? If he finds them different in name at the outset, it becomes easy for him to determine that the name is a shared name; and if he does not find them different in names, then it is difficult for him. For it is not impossible that there be a shared name and opposite to it a shared name, like its opposite. An example of the first is that the name 'sharp' applies to the sword and to sound. It has, in terms of its application to sound, a specific opposite named 'dull [in sound]', and in terms of its application to the sword, a specific opposite named 'blunt'. He finds them different; thus 'sharp' in the sword and sound is by sharing of the name. And so on in other cases. As for the second example, it is that the opposite is one in the shared name, as it is said 'clear sound' and it is said 'muddy sound'; here this law is not beneficial. And there is no deliverance from the aspect of naming except by ambiguity, but rather the definition, quiddity, mark, and property are what indicate in such cases the sharing. For if clarity had one meaning in both, they would be perceived by one sense, but it is not so; rather, one is perceived by hearing, the other by sight. And so on in other cases. These are laws from the aspect of opposition. If the thing has no opposite, but rather by way of opposition it has an opposite according to simple contradiction necessarily. And you know the difference between it and contradiction. If you find the negative expression shared, then similarly the positive expression, like our saying 'non-seeing' and 'seeing' [؟]. For if one of them is shared, then 'seeing' opposite to it is shared in it. And likewise by way of knowledge and privation: if 'sight' is of two kinds – sight of the heart and sight of the eye – then similarly 'blindness'. Also one must consider the conjugations in derivations: if 'justice' is shared in them, then 'just' is shared; and if 'sickness' is shared, then 'sick' is shared. Also, one should elevate the things named by the names to their genera: if their elevation differs, then the name is shared. For 'good' is said of the king, of virtue, and of the mean. The definition of the first elevates to substance, the second to quality, the third to quality; thus the name 'good' then applies to them with different meanings. Unless by 'good' is meant an essential matter such that it would be among the shared names – but 'good' is not like that. For the king is good as a substance complete in existence, having no potentiality. Likewise if they do not elevate to different highest genera but to different intermediate genera, like 'white' in colors and 'white' in sounds, and like 'sharp' in sounds and 'sharp' in angles; they do not elevate to highest genera but to different proximate genera. And so on in other cases. For it may occur from the aspect of genera and species, some above others, that one thing is by sharing in that it indicates the more general and the more specific. Also, one must look at the differentiate of what is indicated by the name: are they different or are they the same differentiate? If you find the differentiate different, then the name is shared. For you find the differentiate of color that is said of the seeing – separating and gathering the seeing – and the differentiate of color that indicates one of the three genera in melodies [؟] – if the differentiate were different, then color is a name.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents a series of logical and linguistic laws (qawanin) for determining whether a name applied to multiple things is shared (mushtarak, i.e., homonymous) or univocal. The author begins by emphasizing that one must not only say that a term like 'good' applies in multiple meanings, but must also explain how those meanings differ (e.g., as quality vs. agent). Then he lists several tests. First, the law of opposites: if the things named have different opposites, the name is likely shared. Example: 'sharp' (had) applied to sword and sound has opposite 'blunt' (kalil) for sword and 'dull' (naql?) for sound, proving sharing. However, if the opposite is the same (e.g., 'clear' and 'muddy' for sound), this test fails, and one must rely on definition, essence, or property. Second, if a name has no direct opposite, one uses the opposite of its negation: if the negative expression is shared, the positive is also shared. Third, consider privation: if sight (basar) has two types (heart and eye), then blindness also has two types, indicating sharing. Fourth, check derivations: if a root noun is shared, its derived forms (e.g., 'justice' -> 'just') are shared. Fifth, elevate the named things to their genera: if they elevate to different highest genera (e.g., substance vs. quality), the name is shared. Example: 'good' said of king (substance), virtue (quality), and the mean (quality) – though the last two share a genus. If they elevate to different intermediate genera (e.g., 'white' in color vs. sound), that also indicates sharing. Sixth, examine the differentiate: if the differentiate of the named things differ, the name is shared. Example: 'color' applied to visible color and musical color has different differentiate. Each law provides a methodical way to resolve ambiguity in terms, crucial for logical analysis and definition.

Key Terms
فصل
اسم مشترك

A) Univocal

D) Ambiguous

Key Takeaway
The law of differentiae provides a precise method: compare the distinguishing features; if they differ, the name is shared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Apply the law of differentiate to the name 'farḍ' (obligatory) in Hanafi fiqh: 'farḍ' is used for an obligation established by definitive evidence, while 'wājib' is for speculative evidence. If someone uses 'farḍ' to cover both, what do the differentiae tell you?

Three Quick Methods to Detect Homonymy: Negation, Privation, Derivation
First part of the passage: negation, privation, derivation methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the methods of negation, possession-privation, and derivation to test whether an Arabic word is homonymous (mushtarak).

Core Idea
If the negative shares multiplicity, the affirmative does too; derivation preserves homonymy.

In this passage, the author presents three straightforward techniques to uncover whether a word carries multiple unrelated meanings. These methods rely on linguistic patterns: the link between affirmative and negative terms, the symmetry between possession and absence, and the consistency across derived forms.

Guided Path
1
Check the negative form
If the negative word (e.g., 'not seeing') has multiple senses, the affirmative ('seeing') also has multiple senses. This is because homonymy in one form implies homonymy in the counterpart.
2
Apply the privation test
If the positive term (like 'sight') has two meanings (heart-sight, eye-sight), the privative term ('blindness') shares those meanings. The presence of multiple senses in the positive indicates the privative also has them.
This is based on the principle that possession and privation are correlates.
3
Trace the derivations
If a root word like 'justice' is shared in its meanings, then its derivatives ('just') are also shared. Similarly for 'sickness' and 'sick'. Derivation does not eliminate homonymy; it transmits it.
4
Synthesize the results
If any of these tests indicate multiplicity, the name is likely homonymous. These methods are quick initial checks before deeper genus/differentia analysis.

الأجناس الثلاثة التي في الألحان جما وبدنا [؟] فإلا كانت الفصول مختلفة كان اللون اسما. ثم النوع لا يكون فصلا البتة لما هو نوع، ولا الفصل نوعا له. وإذا كان كذلك فإن كان أحدهما نوعا، والآخر فصلا كان الاسم مشتركا مثل الأبيض في الألوان فإنه نوع وفي الأصوات فإنه فصل؛ فإذن هما باشتراك الاسم ولكن يجب أن ننظر أن هذا البحث هل هو حقيقي أو بحسب المشهور. فنقول أما ما يقال إن فصول الأعراض هي أنواعها فقد مرَّ فيما سلف أنه لا يجب أن يفهم من ذلك أن الفصل المنطقي للعرض هو بعينه نوعه بل يفهم أن معنى الفصل في كل موضع غير معنى النوع. وإن الجنس ليس البتة جزءا من مفهوم الفصل وهو جزء دائما من مفهوم النوع لكن معنى ذلك أنه ليس يحتاج النوع الذي للبسائط إلى أن يكون له فصل بسيط حتى يكون له فصل منطقي بل الذي هو النوع فيها إنما يكون معنى الفصل المنطقي لذاته نحو البياض وما يجري مجراه من البسائط ليس مفرقًا للبصر بتفرق يقترن به. فيكون بينهما ذو تفريق بل يكون مفرقًا للبصير لذاته لا كالناطق، ويجب أن نعلم الفصل المنطقي لا يكون نوعًا البتة إلا بفصل منطقي آخر؛ وهو الذي يكون له مكان جنس. وما يكون نوع مقولة من المقولات فليس هو فصلا لشيء على أنه فصل منطقي بل يكون فصلا لشيء على أنه فصل بسيط والكلام هنا في الفصل المنطقي فليس شيء من الفصول المنطقية نوعًا لشيء من المقولات، وبالعكس ليس شيء من أنواع المقولات فصلا منطقيا لشيء؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت.
You have known the difference between it and contradiction. If you find the negative word is shared, then likewise the affirmative word, like our saying 'not seeing' and 'does not use seeing'. For if one of them is shared, then 'seeing' in contrast to it is shared in it. Likewise from the manner of possession and privation: if 'sight' is in two ways—sight of heart and sight of eye—then likewise 'blindness'. Also, one must consider the conjugations in derivations: if 'justice' is shared in them, then 'just' is shared; and if 'sickness' is shared, then 'sick' is shared. Also, that you elevate the things named by the names to their genera: if their elevation differs, the name is shared. For if 'good' is said of royalty, and is said of virtue, and is said of equality: the definition of the first elevates to substance, the second to quality, the third to quantity; thus the name 'good' falls upon them with different meanings. Unless 'good' is meant as one of the necessary things so that it would be among the ambivalent names, but 'good' is not like that. For royalty is good as a substance perfect in existence, having no potentiality. And likewise if it does not elevate to different higher genera but to different intermediate genera, such as 'white' in colors and 'white' in sounds, and such as 'sharp' in sounds and 'sharp' in angles; for they do not elevate to higher genera but elevate to different proximate genera. And upon this for other forms: it may occur from the aspect of genera and species that are above one another that the word is shared upon one thing in terms of indicating the more general and the more specific. Also, you must consider the differentiae of what the name indicates: whether they are different or whether they are the very same differentiae. If you find the differentiae different, the name is shared. For you find the differentiae of color that is said of 'seeing' as that which distinguishes 'seeing' and gathers it, and the differentiae of color that indicates one of three genera in melodies...? For if the differentiae were different, color would be a name. Then the species is never a differentia for what is a species, nor is the differentia a species for it. If it is so, then if one is a species and the other is a differentia, the name is shared, like 'white' in colors, for it is a species, and in sounds, for it is a differentia. Thus they are by homonymy, but we must consider whether this investigation is real or according to common usage. So we say: as for what is said that the differentiae of accidents are their species, it has passed before that it should not be understood from that that the logical differentia of an accident is exactly its species, but rather it is understood that the meaning of differentia in each place is other than the meaning of species. And that the genus is never a part of the concept of differentia, and it is always a part of the concept of species, but the meaning of that is that the species that is for simples does not need to have a simple differentia such that it would have a logical differentia; rather that which is the species in them is only the meaning of logical differentia in itself, like whiteness and what runs its course from among simples: it is not distinguishing for vision by a distinction that is conjoined with it, so that there would be between them a distinguishing thing; rather it is distinguishing for vision in itself, not like 'rational'. And it is necessary for you to know that the logical differentia is never a species except with another logical differentia; and it is that which has a place of genus. And whatever is a species of a category is not a differentia for something as a logical differentia, but is a differentia for something as a simple differentia. And the speech here is about the logical differentia, so none of the logical differentiae is a species for any of the categories, and conversely none of the species of categories is a logical differentia for anything; so consider what you have known.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory lesson on how to detect if a name is homonymous (mushtarak). The author begins by linking homonymy in negative and affirmative terms: if a negative word is shared, its affirmative counterpart is also shared. Then he moves to the method via possession and privation (al-'ilm wa al-'adam): if the positive term (like 'sight') has multiple senses, the privative term (like 'blindness') likewise shares those senses. Next, derivation (ishtiqāq): if the root meaning is shared, derivatives are shared. The central method is elevation to genera (raf' ilā ajnās): if the definitions of the things named elevate to different higher genera (e.g., substance, quality, quantity), the name is homonymous. He gives the example of 'good' (al-khayr), which can refer to royalty (substance), virtue (quality), or equality (quantity). He notes that if the name is truly ambiguous (tashkīk) like necessary attributes, it's different, but 'good' is not like that. He also considers the case where things elevate to different proximate genera (e.g., 'white' in color vs. in sound; 'sharp' in sound vs. angle). Then he addresses the method via differentiae (fuṣūl): if the differentiae of the things named are different, the name is shared. He illustrates with 'color' in the context of sight and melody. The author then discusses the relationship between species and differentia, stressing that a species cannot be a differentia for what is that species, and vice versa; if one is a species and the other a differentia, the name is shared. He gives the example of 'white': in colors it is a species, in sounds it is a differentia. He clarifies a subtle point about logical differentia vs. simple differentia: for simple accidents like whiteness, the concept itself serves as a differentia in itself, not through an additional differentia. Finally, he emphasizes that no logical differentia is a species for any category, and vice versa. The passage is a thorough presentation of criteria for homonymy detection.

Key Terms
الاسم المشترك (al-ism al-mushtarak): A name that indicates multiple distinct meanings by pure homonymy (not by analogy or derivation). [Note: In later works, mushtarak is often contrasted with mutawāṭi' (univocal) and mushakkik (ambiguous).]
العلم والعدم (al-'ilm wa al-'adam): Possession and privation: a method to detect homonymy by checking if the privative term shares the multiplicity of the positive term. [Note: This is a standard technique in Arabic logic; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is univocal.
B) It is shared.
C) It is not derived.
D) It is homonymous.

Key Takeaway
Negation, privation, and derivation provide quick linguistic indicators of homonymy.

Detecting Homonymy by Elevating to Genera
Middle of the passage: elevation to genera method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of elevating names to their genera to determine if a word is homonymous, using the example of 'good'.

Core Idea
If definitions elevate to different high genera, the name is homonymous.

The most systematic method for detecting homonymy is to examine the genera under which the named things fall. The author illustrates this with the word 'good' (al-khayr) and its three senses: royalty (substance), virtue (quality), and equality (quantity). Since these belong to different high genera, 'good' is homonymous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the multiple senses of the target word
For 'good', three meanings: royalty, virtue, equality. List them clearly.
2
Find the definition of each sense
Define royalty as 'a substance perfect in existence', virtue as a quality, equality as a quantity. These are the definitions implied by the author.
3
Elevate each definition to its highest genus
Royalty → substance (jawhar); virtue → quality (kayf); equality → quantity (kamm). This is the critical step.
4
Compare the genera
If they differ at the highest genus (like here), the name is homonymous. If they share the same high genus, further check intermediate genera (e.g., 'white' in colors vs. sounds).
5
Consider the exception of ambivalent (mushakkik) names
If the name is truly ambiguous like 'necessary' (wājib), it may still be univocal in a different sense. But 'good' is not like that because it is not a necessary attribute.

وعرفت الفرق بينه وبين التناقض فإن وجدت اللفظة السلبية مشتركة فكذلك اللفظة الإيجابية كقولنا لا بصير ولا يستعمل البصير. فإن أحدهما إذا كان مشتركًا فالبصير بإزائه مشترك فيه. وكذلك من طريق العلم والعدم فإنه إن كان البصر على وجهين؛ بصر قلب وبصر عين فكذلك العمى. وأيضًا يجب أن يعتبر التصاريف في الاشتقاقات؛ فإن العدالة إذا كانت مشتركةً فيها كان العدل مشتركًا، وإن كان المُصح مشتركا كان الصحيح مشتركًا. وأيضًا أن ترفع الأمور المسماة بالأسماء إلى أجناسها؛ فإن اختلف ارتفاعها كان الاسم مشتركًا فإن الخير إذا قيل للملك، وقيل للفضيلة، وقيل للمساوي. وحد الأول يرتقي إلى الجوهر، والثاني إلى الكم، والثالث إلى الكم؛ فيكون اسم الخير إذن يقع عليها بمعان مختلفة. اللهم إلا أن يُعنى بالخير أمر من الأمور اللازمة حتى يكون من الأسماء المشككة إلا أن الخير ليس كذلك. فإن الملك خير على أنه جوهر كامل الوجود، وليس فيه ما بالقوة. وكذلك إن لم يرتفع إلى أجناس عالية مختلفة بل أجناس متوسطة مختلفة مثل الأبيض في الألوان والأبيض في الأصوات، ومثل الحاد من الأصوات والحاد من الزاوية؛ فإنها ليست ترتفع إلى أجناس عالية بل ترتفع إلى أجناس قريبة مختلفة. وعلى هذا في الغير من الصور فإنه قد يعرض من جهة الأجناس والأنواع التي بعضها فوق بعض أن يكون الواحد بالاشتراك على شيء واحد من حيث يدل على الأعم والأخص. وأيضًا يجب أن تنظر في فصول ما يدل عليه بالاسم هل هي مختلفة أو هل هي فصول واحدة بأعيانها؛ فإن وجدت الفصول مختلفة يكون الاسم مشتركًا فإنك تجد فصول اللون الذي يقال على البصير مفرَّقًا للبصير وجامعا له، وفصول اللون الذي يدل على أحد الأجناس الثلاثة التي في الألحان جما وبدنا [؟] فإلا كانت الفصول مختلفة كان اللون اسما. ثم النوع لا يكون فصلا البتة لما هو نوع، ولا الفصل نوعا له. وإذا كان كذلك فإن كان أحدهما نوعا، والآخر فصلا كان الاسم مشتركا مثل الأبيض في الألوان فإنه نوع وفي الأصوات فإنه فصل؛ فإذن هما باشتراك الاسم ولكن يجب أن ننظر أن هذا البحث هل هو حقيقي أو بحسب المشهور. فنقول أما ما يقال إن فصول الأعراض هي أنواعها فقد مرَّ فيما سلف أنه لا يجب أن يفهم من ذلك أن الفصل المنطقي للعرض هو بعينه نوعه بل يفهم أن معنى الفصل في كل موضع غير معنى النوع. وإن الجنس ليس البتة جزءا من مفهوم الفصل وهو جزء دائما من مفهوم النوع لكن معنى ذلك أنه ليس يحتاج النوع الذي للبسائط إلى أن يكون له فصل بسيط حتى يكون له فصل منطقي بل الذي هو النوع فيها إنما يكون معنى الفصل المنطقي لذاته نحو البياض وما يجري مجراه من البسائط ليس مفرقًا للبصر بتفرق يقترن به. فيكون بينهما ذو تفريق بل يكون مفرقًا للبصير لذاته لا كالناطق، ويجب أن نعلم الفصل المنطقي لا يكون نوعًا البتة إلا بفصل منطقي آخر؛ وهو الذي يكون له مكان جنس. وما يكون نوع مقولة من المقولات فليس هو فصلا لشيء على أنه فصل منطقي بل يكون فصلا لشيء على أنه فصل بسيط والكلام هنا في الفصل المنطقي فليس شيء من الفصول المنطقية نوعًا لشيء من المقولات، وبالعكس ليس شيء من أنواع المقولات فصلا منطقيا لشيء؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت.
You have known the difference between it and contradiction. If you find the negative word is shared, then likewise the affirmative word, like our saying 'not seeing' and 'does not use seeing'. For if one of them is shared, then 'seeing' in contrast to it is shared in it. Likewise from the manner of possession and privation: if 'sight' is in two ways—sight of heart and sight of eye—then likewise 'blindness'. Also, one must consider the conjugations in derivations: if 'justice' is shared in them, then 'just' is shared; and if 'sickness' is shared, then 'sick' is shared. Also, that you elevate the things named by the names to their genera: if their elevation differs, the name is shared. For if 'good' is said of royalty, and is said of virtue, and is said of equality: the definition of the first elevates to substance, the second to quality, the third to quantity; thus the name 'good' falls upon them with different meanings. Unless 'good' is meant as one of the necessary things so that it would be among the ambivalent names, but 'good' is not like that. For royalty is good as a substance perfect in existence, having no potentiality. And likewise if it does not elevate to different higher genera but to different intermediate genera, such as 'white' in colors and 'white' in sounds, and such as 'sharp' in sounds and 'sharp' in angles; for they do not elevate to higher genera but elevate to different proximate genera. And upon this for other forms: it may occur from the aspect of genera and species that are above one another that the word is shared upon one thing in terms of indicating the more general and the more specific. Also, you must consider the differentiae of what the name indicates: whether they are different or whether they are the very same differentiae. If you find the differentiae different, the name is shared. For you find the differentiae of color that is said of 'seeing' as that which distinguishes 'seeing' and gathers it, and the differentiae of color that indicates one of three genera in melodies...? For if the differentiae were different, color would be a name. Then the species is never a differentia for what is a species, nor is the differentia a species for it. If it is so, then if one is a species and the other is a differentia, the name is shared, like 'white' in colors, for it is a species, and in sounds, for it is a differentia. Thus they are by homonymy, but we must consider whether this investigation is real or according to common usage. So we say: as for what is said that the differentiae of accidents are their species, it has passed before that it should not be understood from that that the logical differentia of an accident is exactly its species, but rather it is understood that the meaning of differentia in each place is other than the meaning of species. And that the genus is never a part of the concept of differentia, and it is always a part of the concept of species, but the meaning of that is that the species that is for simples does not need to have a simple differentia such that it would have a logical differentia; rather that which is the species in them is only the meaning of logical differentia in itself, like whiteness and what runs its course from among simples: it is not distinguishing for vision by a distinction that is conjoined with it, so that there would be between them a distinguishing thing; rather it is distinguishing for vision in itself, not like 'rational'. And it is necessary for you to know that the logical differentia is never a species except with another logical differentia; and it is that which has a place of genus. And whatever is a species of a category is not a differentia for something as a logical differentia, but is a differentia for something as a simple differentia. And the speech here is about the logical differentia, so none of the logical differentiae is a species for any of the categories, and conversely none of the species of categories is a logical differentia for anything; so consider what you have known.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory lesson on how to detect if a name is homonymous (mushtarak). The author begins by linking homonymy in negative and affirmative terms: if a negative word is shared, its affirmative counterpart is also shared. Then he moves to the method via possession and privation (al-'ilm wa al-'adam): if the positive term (like 'sight') has multiple senses, the privative term (like 'blindness') likewise shares those senses. Next, derivation (ishtiqāq): if the root meaning is shared, derivatives are shared. The central method is elevation to genera (raf' ilā ajnās): if the definitions of the things named elevate to different higher genera (e.g., substance, quality, quantity), the name is homonymous. He gives the example of 'good' (al-khayr), which can refer to royalty (substance), virtue (quality), or equality (quantity). He notes that if the name is truly ambiguous (tashkīk) like necessary attributes, it's different, but 'good' is not like that. He also considers the case where things elevate to different proximate genera (e.g., 'white' in color vs. in sound; 'sharp' in sound vs. angle). Then he addresses the method via differentiae (fuṣūl): if the differentiae of the things named are different, the name is shared. He illustrates with 'color' in the context of sight and melody. The author then discusses the relationship between species and differentia, stressing that a species cannot be a differentia for what is that species, and vice versa; if one is a species and the other a differentia, the name is shared. He gives the example of 'white': in colors it is a species, in sounds it is a differentia. He clarifies a subtle point about logical differentia vs. simple differentia: for simple accidents like whiteness, the concept itself serves as a differentia in itself, not through an additional differentia. Finally, he emphasizes that no logical differentia is a species for any category, and vice versa. The passage is a thorough presentation of criteria for homonymy detection.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): The genus in the logical sense: a universal predicated of many things differing in species, used here to check if definitions belong to different genera. [Note: Stable concept, but sometimes used for high genera (ajnas 'āliya) vs. low.]
الاسم المشترك (al-ism al-mushtarak): A name that indicates multiple distinct meanings by pure homonymy (not by analogy or derivation). [Note: In later works, mushtarak is often contrasted with mutawāṭi' (univocal) and mushakkik (ambiguous).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why is 'good' considered homonymous?
A) Because it has multiple meanings.
B) Because its definitions elevate to different genera.
C) Because it is an ambivalent name.
D) Because it is not a necessary attribute.

Key Takeaway
Elevating definitions to their genera reveals homonymy when the genera differ at any level.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the elevation-to-genera test to the word 'ḥarakah' (movement) which can mean motion in physics (change of place) and motion in grammar (vowel). Do they elevate to the same genus? If not, what does that imply?
?
The word 'qalb' (heart) can mean the physical organ and the centre of emotion. Elevate each to its genus. Are they the same or different? Is 'qalb' homonymous?

Detecting Homonymy through Differentiae and the Species-Differentia Rule
Later part of the passage: differentiae and species-differentia relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the differentia test and the rule that a species cannot be a differentia for itself (and vice versa) to identify homonymous names.

Core Idea
If differentiae differ or if one sense is a species and another is a differentia, the name is homonymous.

The author presents a more refined method: examine the differentiae (fuṣūl) of the things named. If the differentiae are different, the name is shared. Moreover, if one sense is a species and another is a differentia, they cannot be the same meaning, indicating homonymy. The example 'white' in colors (species) vs. in sounds (differentia) illustrates this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the differentiae of each meaning
For 'color' in sight and melody, check the differentiae that distinguish each. The author mentions 'the differentiae of color that is said of seeing' and 'differentiae of color that indicates one of three genera in melodies'.
2
Compare the differentiae
If they are different, the name is homonymous. Example: 'seeing' in vision vs. 'seeing' in understanding have different differentiae.
3
Apply the species-differentia rule
If one meaning is a species (e.g., 'white' as a color) and another is a differentia (e.g., 'white' as a sound quality), the name is homonymous because a species cannot be a differentia for itself.
4
Distinguish logical differentia from simple differentia
For simple accidents like whiteness, the concept itself serves as a differentia without needing an additional differentia. But logical differentia is never a species for any category.

Source Text

You have known the difference between it and contradiction. If you find the negative word is shared, then likewise the affirmative word, like our saying 'not seeing' and 'does not use seeing'. Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory lesson on how to detect if a name is homonymous (mushtarak). The author begins by linking homonymy in negative and affirmative terms: if a negative word is shared, its affirmative counterpart is also shared. Then he moves to the method via possession and privation (al-'ilm wa al-'adam): if the positive term (like 'sight') has multiple senses, the privative term (like 'blindness') likewise shares those senses. Next, derivation (ishtiqāq): if the root meaning is shared, derivatives are shared. The central method is elevation to genera (raf' ilā ajnās): if the definitions of the things named elevate to different higher genera (e.g., substance, quality, quantity), the name is homonymous. He gives the example of 'good' (al-khayr), which can refer to royalty (substance), virtue (quality), or equality (quantity). He notes that if the name is truly ambiguous (tashkīk) like necessary attributes, it's different, but 'good' is not like that. He also considers the case where things elevate to different proximate genera (e.g., 'white' in color vs. in sound; 'sharp' in sound vs. angle). Then he addresses the method via differentiae (fuṣūl): if the differentiae of the things named are different, the name is shared. He illustrates with 'color' in the context of sight and melody. The author then discusses the relationship between species and differentia, stressing that a species cannot be a differentia for what is that species, and vice versa; if one is a species and the other a differentia, the name is shared. He gives the example of 'white': in colors it is a species, in sounds it is a differentia. Finally, he emphasizes that no logical differentia is a species for any category, and vice versa. The passage is a thorough presentation of criteria for homonymy detection.

Key Terms
الفصل المنطقي (al-faṣl al-manṭiqī): The logical differentia: a distinctive attribute that constitutes the species, as opposed to a simple differentia found in simple accidents. [Note: This text distinguishes logical differentia from simple differentia; not all later texts maintain this sharp distinction.]
النوع (al-nawʿ): Species: the second of the five universals, a universal predicated of many differing in number. [Note: Stable concept.]

According to the passage, what is the relationship between 'white' in colors and 'white' in sounds?
A) They share the same differentia.
B) One is a species and the other is a differentia.
C) Both are differentiae.
D) Both are species.

Key Takeaway
Different differentiae or a species-differentia mismatch reveal homonymy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'ṣūrah' (form) which can mean the external shape (species) and the internal meaning (like a concept, differentia). Apply the differentia test: are the differentiae of these two senses the same or different? Conclude on homonymy.
?

The Third Instrument: Deriving Differentiae
Author introduces the third logical tool: skill in extracting differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose and limitation of the third logical instrument and identify when it is needed in Islamic reasoning.

Core Idea
Deriving differentiae distinguishes things that share similar judgments.

In logic, we need tools not only to define terms but also to distinguish between things that appear similar. The third instrument addresses precisely this: the skill of extracting differentiae (fuṣūl) from things. The author warns that this skill requires practice and can fail when things are truly homogeneous. In this lesson, we explore what this instrument is and why it matters in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tool being described
Read the passage and locate the description of the third instrument. What is its function? How is it described?
The passage says it is 'the ability to derive differentiae from things' (al-iqtidār 'alā istinbāṭ al-fuṣūl).
2
Understand its purpose
Why is this tool needed? According to the passage, it is used 'to distinguish between things with similar judgments' (li-tafrīq bayn al-umūr al-mutashābiha al-aḥkām).
So it helps when things appear similar in their rulings or attributes.
3
Recognize its limitation
The author states that this skill fails (batalat) when it comes to distinguishing truly homogeneous things (mutajānisa) or things similar in definition. This means the tool has boundaries: it works for things similar in judgment but not for things that are essentially identical.
This is a warning not to overapply the tool.

Commentary
This passage introduces the third instrument (al-āla al-thālitha) of logic, which is the capacity to derive differentiae (fuṣūl) from things. The author states that this skill is essential for distinguishing between objects that share similar judgments (mutashābihāt al-aḥkām). The best application of this instrument is in considering the judgments of a single thing, and he notes that using analogy (qiyās) with things of similar genera is very beneficial, for instance in distinguishing between judgments of the senses. The passage emphasizes the practical utility of this tool in logical analysis, particularly in situations where things appear similar but need to be differentiated.

The Logical Differentia Cannot Be a Species
Author clarifies the relationship between logical differentia and species, addressing homonymy and simple accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a logical differentia and a species and identify cases of homonymy.

Core Idea
A logical differentia is never a species; apparent cases are homonymy, not real identity.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial distinction: the logical differentia (faṣl manṭiqī) and the species (nawʿ) are never the same thing. Sometimes they appear to be, but that is only due to homonymy (ishtirāk al-ism). Understanding this prevents confusion when defining terms in Islamic logic and other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Find the statement where the author says a logical differentia is never a species, and vice versa. This is the central rule.
The rule is absolute; no exceptions in logical terms.
2
Analyze the homonymy example
Consider the example of 'white' (al-abyad): it is a species among colors but a differentia among sounds. Why does this not break the rule? Because the word is used homonymously, not logically.
Homonymy means same word, different meanings.
3
Distinguish real from conventional
The author asks whether the investigation is real (ḥaqīqī) or according to common usage (mashhūr). This helps explain why some apparent counterexamples arise due to loose speech.
In logical reality, differentia and species are distinct.
4
Examine differentia of simple accidents
For simple things like whiteness, the species itself functions as the differentia because there is no separate distinguishing feature. But this does not make the differentia a species in the logical sense.
This is a special case; the logical differentia still differs from species.
5
Apply to categories
The author concludes that no logical differentia is a species for any category, and no species of a category is a logical differentia. Confirm this with the text.
This reinforces the strict separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكون فصلا البتة لما هو نوع ، ولا الفصل نوعا له. وإذا كان كذلك فإن كان أحدهما نوعا، والآخر فصلا كان الاسم مشتركا مثل الأبيض في الألوان فإنه نوع وفي الأصوات فإنه فصل ؛ فإذن هما باشتراك الاسم ولكن يجب أن ننظر أن هذا البحث هل هو حقيقي أو بحسب المشهور. فنقول أما ما يقال إن فصول الأعراض هي أنواعها فقد مرَّ فيما سلف أنه لا يجب أن يفهم من ذلك أن الفصل المنطقي للعرض هو بعينه نوعه بل يفهم أن معنى الفصل في كل موضع غير معنى النوع. وإن الجنس ليس البتة جزءا من مفهوم الفصل وهو جزء دائما من مفهوم النوع لكن معنى ذلك أنه ليس يحتاج النوع الذي للبسائط إلى أن يكون له فصل بسيط حتى يكون له فصل منطقي بل الذي هو النوع فيها إنما يكون معنى الفصل المنطقي لذاته نحو البياض؛ وما يجري مجراه من البسائط ليس مفرّقًا للبصر بتفرُّق يقترن به. فيكون بينهما ذو تفريق بل يكون مفرّقاً للبصير لذاته لا كالناطق، ويجب أن نعلم الفصل المنطقي لا يكون نوعًا البتة إلا بفصل منطقي آخر؛ وهو الذي يكون له مكان جنس. وما يكون نوع مقولة من المقولات فليس هو فصلا لشيء على أنه فصل منطقي بل يكون فصلا لشيء على أنه فصل بسيط والكلام هنا في الفصل المنطقي فليس شيء من الفصول المنطقية نوعًا لشيء من المقولات، وبالعكس ليس شيء من أنواع المقولات فصلا منطقيا لشيء؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت.
It is never a differentia for that which is a species, nor is the differentia a species for it. And if it is such that one is a species and the other a differentia, then the name is homonymous, like 'white' in colors - it is a species, and in sounds - it is a differentia. So they are by homonymy. But we must consider whether this investigation is real or according to common usage. We say: as for what is said that the differentiae of accidents are their species, it has passed earlier that it should not be understood from that that the logical differentia of an accident is exactly its species; rather it is understood that the meaning of differentia in each place is other than the meaning of species. And the genus is never a part of the concept of differentia, while it is always a part of the concept of species. But the meaning of that is that the species that belongs to simple things does not need to have a simple differentia so that it has a logical differentia; rather that which is the species in them is the meaning of the logical differentia itself, like whiteness. And what runs its course among simple things is not distinguishing to the sight by a distinction accompanying it; so there is between them something that distinguishes, but it distinguishes to the insight essentially, not like 'rational'. And we must know that the logical differentia is never a species except by another logical differentia; and it is that which has the place of genus. And whatever is the species of a category is not a differentia for something as a logical differentia, but it is a differentia for something as a simple differentia. And the talk here is about the logical differentia. So none of the logical differentiae is a species for any of the categories, and conversely none of the species of categories is a logical differentia for anything. So consider what you have known.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the relationship between the logical differentia (al-faṣl al-manṭiqī) and species (al-nawʿ). The author begins by stating a strict rule: a differentia can never be a differentia for what is itself a species, nor can a differentia be a species for that thing. If it appears that one is a species and the other a differentia, it is only by homonymy (ishtirāk al-ism), as the word 'white' (al-abyad) is a species among colors but a differentia among sounds. The author then distinguishes between real (ḥaqīqī) and conventional (bi-ḥasab al-mashhūr) usage. Concerning the claim that the differentiae of accidents are their species, the author clarifies that this does not mean the logical differentia of an accident is identical to its species; rather, the meaning of differentia differs from the meaning of species. The genus (al-jins) is never a part of the concept of a differentia, but it is always a part of the concept of a species. For simple accidents (al-basāʾiṭ) like whiteness, the species itself functions as the logical differentia because there is no separate distinguishing feature. The logical differentia distinguishes essentially (li-dhātih), not like 'rational' which distinguishes by a distinct concept. Finally, the author emphasizes that a logical differentia can only become a species if it itself has another logical differentia, and it then occupies the place of a genus. Species of categories are not logical differentiae for anything; they are only simple differentiae. The entire discussion is about the logical differentia, not the simple differentia, so no logical differentia is a species for any category, and vice versa.

نوع
جنس
اشتراك الاسم

Key Takeaway
A logical differentia can never be a species; apparent cases are homonymy, and the distinction is essential for correct definition.

The Third Instrument: Deriving Differentiae
Defines the third logical instrument as the ability to derive differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope and application of the third logical instrument (istiqbāṭ al-fuṣūl) and distinguish when it is properly used versus misapplied.

Core Idea
The third instrument distinguishes things with similar rulings; misapplied on the homogeneous or dissimilar.

The third instrument is the skill of deriving differentiae. It is crucial for distinguishing things that appear similar but have different rulings. However, it has a specific domain: it works best on things with similar genera or rulings, not on things that are already homogeneous or too dissimilar. This lesson clarifies its proper use.

Guided Path
1
Define the instrument
The passage calls it 'the third instrument' (ālā thālitha) and defines it as the ability to derive differentiae (istiqbāṭ al-fuṣūl) from things. Recognize that this is a logical skill requiring practice.
Differentiae are distinguishing characteristics that separate one species from another within a genus.
2
Identify its purpose
The purpose is to distinguish between things that have similar rulings (al-umūr al-mutashābiha al-aḥkām). It is used when things share a common ruling but need to be differentiated.
Example: differentiating between sensation (ḥiss) and knowledge (ʿilm) which have similar rulings in some contexts.
3
Recognize misapplication
The faculty becomes spoiled (batalat) when used to distinguish between homogeneous things (mutajānisa) or things similar in definition (mutashābiha ḥaddan). That is, when the things are already clearly distinct or too similar, the instrument is unnecessary or counterproductive.
Misuse leads to confusion, not clarity.
4
Apply the proper domain
The best use is in considering rulings of one thing (iʿtibār aḥkām shayʾ wāḥid) and using analogy (qiyās) for things with similar genera. The passage gives the example of distinguishing between rulings of sensation and knowledge.
This aligns with fiqh where similar cases are compared via analogy.
5
Identify the improper domain
For things far apart in definition, with different genera, no similarity in natures, genera, or subjects, considering the difference is unproductive – like a basin (ḥawḍ) in knowing a known matter.
Do not apply the third instrument to utterly dissimilar things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآلة الثالثة فهي الاقتدار على استنباط الفصول من الأشياء؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا بد من الارتياض به وفيه فإنه بذلك مفرّق بين الأمور المتشابهة الأحكام. وبطلت هذه الملكة من التفريق بين الأمور المتجانسة بل المتشابهة حدا. وأفضل ذلك ما يكون في اعتبارات أحكام شيء واحد على أن البحث عن قياس أمور متشابهة الأجناس نافع جدا مثل التفريق بين أحكام الحس وأحكام العلم. وأما الأشياء المتباعدة حدا المختلفة الأجناس التي لا تشابه فيه في طبائعها ولا في أجناسها ولا موضوعاتها. فإن اعتبار الفرق بينها كأنه حوض في تعرف أمر معلوم؛ وذلك غير مقتدر به.
As for the third instrument, it is the ability to derive differentiae from things; and it is among what must be practiced with and in it, for by it one distinguishes between things with similar rulings. And this faculty becomes spoiled by distinguishing between homogeneous things, rather between things similar in definition. The best of that is in considering the rulings of one thing, and the investigation by analogy of things with similar genera is very beneficial, such as distinguishing between the rulings of sensation and the rulings of knowledge. As for things far apart in definition, with different genera, having no similarity in their natures, genera, or subjects, then considering the difference between them is like a basin in knowing a known matter; and that is not accomplished.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage defines the third logical instrument (al-āla al-thālitha), which is the ability to derive differentiae (istiqbāṭ al-fuṣūl) from things. The author explains that this skill requires practice, as it enables one to distinguish between things that share similar rulings. The misuse of this faculty occurs when one tries to distinguish between homogeneous things or things similar in definition. The best application is in examining the rulings of a single thing and using analogy (qiyās) for things with similar genera, illustrated by the distinction between the rulings of sensation (al-ḥiss) and knowledge (al-ʿilm). However, for things far apart in definition, with different genera and no similarity, considering their differences is unproductive.

Key Terms
آلة ثالثة
استنباط الفصول
مفرّق بين الأمور المتشابهة الأحكام

Key Takeaway
The third instrument is a powerful tool for differentiation, but it must be restricted to things that share a common genus or similar rulings to be effective.

The Fourth Instrument: Gathering Similars
Defines the fourth logical instrument as the ability to find similarities among things with distant genera and different definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of similarity (single meaning vs. proportion) across things with distant genera, and apply this instrument to examples from Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Core Idea
The fourth instrument trains the mind to find hidden commonalities between seemingly unrelated things.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth logical instrument: the ability to gather similars (mutashābihāt). This is not about obvious similarities; it is about spotting commonalities across distant categories. The logician distinguishes between a single meaning shared by two different things (like substance and quantity both lacking an opposite) and a proportional relationship (like sensation to sensed equals knowledge to known). We will learn to classify these types and see how this skill benefits reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core definition
Find the sentence that defines the fourth instrument. Identify its genus (iqtidār – ability) and differentia (akhdh al-mutashābihāt – taking similars) with the condition of distant genera and different definitions.
The definition is: 'وأما الآلة الرابعة فهي الاقتدار على أخذ المتشابهات'.
2
Identify the two species of similarity
The author gives two types: a single meaning (ma'nā mufrad) shared by two things, like substance and quantity both having no opposite, and a proportional relation (nisba) like an analogy. Match each example in the passage to its type.
Substance/quantity → single meaning; sensation/knowledge → proportion.
3
Analyze the proportional subtypes
The passage divides proportional similarities into three cases: no common term, a common term that is relative in both, or relative in one and relative-to in the other. Diagram these with symbols (A:B :: C:D) and map the author's example onto one case.
Example: 'نسبة الحس إلى المحسوس هي نسبة العلم إلى المعلوم' fits the second case.
4
Apply to Islamic science
Consider an example from usul al-fiqh: the analogy between the prohibition of riba and gharar. Identify whether the similarity is a single meaning (e.g., both are prohibited due to exploitation) or proportional. Reflect on how this instrument helps construct valid qiyas.
Single meaning: both involve unjust enrichment; proportional: the relation of riba to exploitation is like gharar to exploitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآلة الرابعة فهي الاقتدار على أخذ المتشابهات؛ ويجب أن يكون ذلك متطلبا في الأشياء البعيدة الأجناس المختلفة حدا. فمن ذلك يكون معنى مفرد مثل تشابه الجوهر والكم في أنهما من جملة ما لا ضد له ومنه ما يكون بالنسبة كما يقال إن نسبة الحس إلى المحسوس هي نسبة العلم إلى المعلوم أو نسبة البياض إلى البصر كنسبة السواد إليه، ونسبة العقل إلى الخيال كنسبة الخيال إلى الحس. وبالجملة إما أن لا يكون في الحدود مشترك، أو يكون مشترك واحد هو في كليهما منسوب أو منسوب إليه، أو في أحدهما منسوب وفي الآخر منسوب إليه، ويجب أن يكون هذا كله في الأشياء المتباعدة. وأيضًا فإن الأشياء المتجانسة إذا كانت بعد التجانس متباينة حدا كالإنسان والفرس وكالبياض والسواد، فإن ارتياد ما يجمعهما بعد التجانس نافع في الدربة.
As for the fourth instrument, it is the ability to take similar things; and that must be sought in things with distant genera and different definitions. From that may be a single meaning, like the similarity of substance and quantity in that they are among what has no opposite. And from it may be by proportion, as it is said: the relation of sensation to the sensed is like the relation of knowledge to the known, or the relation of whiteness to sight is like the relation of blackness to it, and the relation of intellect to imagination is like the relation of imagination to sensation. In summary, either there is no common thing in the definitions, or there is one common thing that in both is relative or relative to, or in one is relative and in the other relative to. And all this must be in distant things. Also, homogeneous things, if after homogeneity they are different in definition like man and horse, and like whiteness and blackness, then seeking what unites them after homogeneity is beneficial for practice.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth logical instrument, which is the ability to gather similar things (akhdh al-mutashābihāt) across distant genera. The author explains that this is sought in things with different definitions and genera. Examples include a single meaning shared by substance and quantity (both have no opposite) or proportional relationships like the analogy between sensation and knowledge. The author categorizes the types of commonality: either no common term in definitions, or a common term that is relative in both or relative in one and related to the other. Additionally, for homogeneous things that differ in definition, finding their common ground is beneficial for practice.

Key Terms
آلة رابعة (āla rābiʿa): The fourth instrument – the skill of identifying similarities across disparate categories.
متشابهات (mutashābihāt): Similar things – things sharing a common aspect despite different definitions.
نسبة (nisba): Proportion – a type of similarity expressed as a relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Sensation and knowledge are both faculties.
D) Whiteness and blackness are both colors.

Key Takeaway
The fourth logical instrument trains the mind to spot hidden commonalities across distant categories, which is essential for constructing valid analogies in Islamic law and theology.

The Nuanced Difference Between Synonymous Terms
Analyzing the nuance between al-shujā‘ and jayyid al-nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two terms are not fully synonymous and explain how their difference in connotation affects logical judgments.

Core Idea
A slight difference in connotation can change a ruling, even if terms seem interchangeable.

In Islamic logic, words that appear to mean the same thing may carry hidden nuances. The author gives the example of 'al-shujā‘' (the brave) and 'jayyid al-nafs' (good-natured): they seem synonymous, but 'al-shujā‘' implies an extra quality that affects judgment. Recognizing such nuances prevents deception in debate and helps you dissect arguments with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pair of terms
Find the two terms in the passage that are being compared: 'al-shujā‘' (الشجاع) and 'jayyid al-nafs' (جيد النفس). Note that the author considers them similar but not identical.
2
Determine the shared meaning
What do they both imply? Both indicate a certain moral quality. 'Jayyid al-nafs' means good-natured, while 'al-shujā‘' also includes that but adds bravery.
3
Identify the additional connotation
The passage states that 'al-shujā‘' suggests something more than just being good-natured. That extra element (bravery, perhaps a physical attribute) is what distinguishes the two.
The extra connotation is explicitly mentioned: 'يوهم شيئًا زائدا'.
4
Assess the impact on judgment
Why does this matter? If a ruling depends on the exact nuance, using 'al-shujā‘' may bring in an unintended implication. The author says: 'وفي تلك الزيادة يعتبر في الحكم' (and in that addition, it is considered in the judgment).
Think of a legal context: if bravery is required, 'jayyid al-nafs' alone may not suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباين للمعنى بوجه فإنه أنقص منه، أو أزيد عليه، أو هو شرط دل عليه وعرف؛ وهذا كما يؤخذ الشجاع وجيد النفس على أن المفهوم منهما واحد، وعلى أن جيد النفس هو قول تحت اسم الشجاع، وعلى أن الكلام فيهما واحد ثمَّ يتأدى به الاسمان في النظر إلى الاشتغال بأحدهما من حيث له زيادة اعتبار بأن استعمال أحدهما يكفي فيما نحن فيه أو لا يكفي؛ فإن لفظ الشجاع يوهم شيئًا زائدا على أنه جيد النفس فقط. وفي تلك الزيادة يعتبر في الحكم؛ وهذا الموضع نافع في دفع التلبيس وسرعة الكشف. وموضع آخر ليس يعتبر فيه الوجود؛ وذلك لأن الشيء كونه موجودًا للموضوع غير كونه له دائمًا أو أكثريا أو أقليا، وغير أنه لكله أو لبعضه، وغير كونه له بالنسبة إلى كذا أو ليس إذا سلم وجود فقد سلم من كل وجه فيجب أن نراعي في ذلك أن لا نأخذ أحد الحكمين مكان الآخر، وأن لا نمنع بمطلق غير مبين بل نطالب القابل المجمل بالبيان ومما يشبه هذا الموضع قول من قال إن من اللذات ما هو فرح، ومنه ما هو سرور، ومنه ما هو خذل؛ وهذه كلها ألفاظ مترادفة لا بد من الاستكشاف.
Differing from the meaning in some aspect, for it is less than it, or more than it, or it is a condition indicated and known. This is like when 'the brave' and 'good-natured' are taken such that the concept of both is one, and that 'good-natured' is a statement under the name 'the brave', and that the speech concerning them is one. Then the two names lead in consideration to preoccupation with one of them insofar as it has an added consideration—whether using one suffices in our matter or not. For the word 'the brave' suggests something additional to its being merely good-natured. And in that addition, it is considered in the judgment. This place is beneficial in repelling deception and speed of discovery. Another place where existence is not considered—because something's being existent for the subject is not the same as its being for it always, or mostly, or rarely, nor its being for all of it or some of it, nor its being for it in relation to such-and-such or not. If existence is granted, then it is granted in every respect. So we must observe in that not to take one judgment in place of the other, and not to refuse with an absolute unexplained [objection], but rather demand from the vague opponent clarification. And among what resembles this is the saying of someone who said: 'Among pleasures, some are joy, some are delight, and some are disgrace.' All these are synonymous words; investigation is necessary.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage defines a situation where two terms appear synonymous but actually differ in nuance, leading to potential deception in argumentation. The author uses the example of 'الشجاع' (the brave) and 'جيد النفس' (good-natured). Although they might seem to have the same concept, 'جيد النفس' is actually a subtype under الشجاع, but الشجاع has an additional connotation beyond mere good nature. This extra connotation affects judgments. The passage then discusses another case where existence is not the issue—rather the manner of existence (always, mostly, rarely, etc.) or the scope (all or some) matters. The key lesson is not to conflate different judgments and to demand clarification when an opponent uses vague terms. The author illustrates with the example of synonyms like joy, delight, and disgrace, all of which are often used interchangeably but require investigation to discern their precise meanings. This is a definitional expansion probe because it explains how to handle terms in logic and dialectic.

Avoiding Conflation of Judgments in Argumentation
Explaining when existence is not the only aspect to consider

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between different aspects of a term (existence, frequency, scope) and demand clarification to avoid conflating judgments.

Core Idea
Existence is not the same as manner, scope, or relation; conflating them leads to fallacious conclusions.

The passage continues by explaining another source of deception: confusing the existence of a property with its manner (always, mostly, rarely), its scope (all or some), or its relation. The author warns not to take one judgment for another and to demand clarification when an opponent uses vague terms. This distinction is crucial in kalam and fiqh arguments where precision is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between the case of term nuance (الشجاع and جيد النفس) and another case where existence vs. manner/scope/relation matters. Recognize that these are different issues.
2
List the aspects not to be conflated
The author lists: وجود (existence) vs. دوام (perpetuity), أكثرية (majority), أقلية (minority); also whether it applies to all (كله) or some (بعضه); also whether it is in relation to something (بالنسبة إلى كذا) or not.
These are distinct modal and quantitative aspects.
3
Understand the fallacy
If an opponent says 'Zaid is generoTTS', and you grant that he exists, it does not mean you grant he is always generous, or generous to all, or generous to the poor. The fallacy is to treat a basic existence claim as if it covers all these details.
The author says: 'إذا سلم وجود فقد سلم من كل وجه' (if existence is granted, then it is granted in every respect) – but that is false; existence is only one aspect.
4
Apply the rule of clarification
The rule: do not accept a judgment in place of another. If an opponent makes a vague claim, demand that he specify which aspect he means. The author says: 'نطالب القابل المجمل بالبيان' (demand from the vague opponent clarification).
This is a key dialectical tool: pin down exactly what is being claimed.
