⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في مثل ذلك ومن المواضع التي يبطل بها ما يوضع جنسًا أن تكون الملكة جعلت جنسا للفعل، والفعل جعل جنسًا للملكة مثل من يقول : إن الحسّ حركة جسمانية؛ والحركة فعل لا مبدأ فعل والحس مبدأ فعل، أو يقول إن التذكر ملكة نفسانية، والملكة النفسانية بحال ثبات لا بحال تجدد وفعل، والتذكر بحال تجدد لا بحال ثبات. ثم الجنس لم يكن فيما فيه النوع بل النوع في غير ما فيه الجنس فليس الجنس جنسًا مثل من يقول إن للحياء جوف ما لكن الجوف للحيواني من قوة النفس الحيوانية، والحياء في النطقية. وكذلك من يقول إن الغيظ ألم أو غم فإن الغيظ في الغضبية، والألم في الحس والغم في الشهوانية أو في السائسة. وبالجملة فإن من الأشياء ما يوهم أنه جنس لغيره؛ وذلك لا يكون جنسا في الحقيقة بل لا يمكن كما أن جزء الشيء إذا نسب إليه الشيء يوهم أن يكون ذلك الجزء جنسًا لذلك الشيء كما في المشهور أن الجسم جنس للإنسان وهو جزؤه: وفرق بين الجسم الذي هو جزء الإنسان وبين الجسم الذي هو جنس الإنسان. وكيف لا وقد يحمل أحدهما على الإنسان دون الآخر على ما عرف في بيان حمل الشيء على الشيء. وكذلك فرق بين الجسم الذي هو جزء القوام والجسم الذي هو جزء الحد؛ فيعرف صحة هذا الموضع ويعلم أن الجسم هنا ليس الجسم الذي هو جزء الحد بل الذي هو جزء القوام.
Section: On something like that. Among the places by which what is placed as a genus is invalidated are: that disposition is made a genus for act, and act is made a genus for disposition, like one who says that sensation is a bodily motion; motion is an act, not a principle of act, while sensation is a principle of act. Or one says that recollection is a psychic disposition, and the psychic disposition is in a state of stability, not in a state of renewal and act, while recollection is in a state of renewal, not in a state of stability. Then the genus is not in that in which the species is, rather the species is in that which is not the genus's location, so it is not a genus. For example, one says that modesty (al-haya') has a cavity (jawf) but the cavity belongs to the animal (al-hayawani) from the power of the animal soul, while modesty is in the rational [soul]. Similarly, one who says that rage is pain or grief, whereas rage is in the irascible [power], pain is in the sensitive, and grief is in the appetitive or in the ruling [power]. In sum, among things there are those that give the illusion that they are a genus for something else, but that is not a genus in reality; indeed it is not possible. Just as a part of a thing, when the thing is related to it, gives the illusion that that part is a genus for that thing, as in the well-known [example] that body is a genus for human while it is a part of him. And there is a difference between the body that is a part of human and the body that is the genus of human. How could it not be, when one of them is predicated of human differently from the other, as is known in the treatise on predication? Likewise, a distinction is made between the body that is a part of substance (juz' al-qiwām) and the body that is a part of the definition (juz' al-ḥadd); thus the correctness of this place is known, and it is known that body here is not the body that is a part of the definition but rather the body that is a part of substance.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This section discusses invalid genus assignments, specifically when a property is mistakenly taken as a genus for another due to superficial commonality. The author gives several examples: (1) Making disposition (malaka) a genus for act (fi'l) or vice versa; e.g., sensation is a bodily motion—motion is an act, but sensation is a principle of act, not an act itself. (2) Recollection is a psychic disposition, but dispositions are stable while recollection is a process of renewal. (3) Modesty (haya') supposedly has a cavity, but cavity belongs to the animal soul, while modesty is in the rational soul—the genus does not contain the species. (4) Rage is said to be pain or grief, but these occur in different faculties. The overarching point: many things give the illusion of being a genus, like body being called the genus of human, when in reality body is a part of human's substance, not a genus. The author emphasizes the crucial distinction between body as a part of the definition (juz' al-ḥadd) and body as a material part (juz' al-qiwām). This clarifies that when we say 'body is a genus', we mean the abstract corporeality, not the physical matter.

When defining something, ensure that what you call a genus is truly a logical category, not a material part or a property of the species.

How to Invalidate a False Genus: Malaka, Fi'l, and Faculty Mismatch
Author invalidates false genus claims using malaka/fi'l and faculty mismatches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid genus claims where the genus and species belong to different faculties or where a disposition is confused with an act.

Core Idea
Malaka and fi'l cannot be each other's genus; genus and species must share the same faculty.

In Islamic logic, assigning a true genus is crucial for definition. The author points out common mistakes: treating a stable disposition (malaka) as the genus of an act, or vice versa, and assigning a genus from a different psychic faculty. This lesson equips you with criteria to detect such errors using examples from the faculties of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claimed Genus
Find the assertion that a certain universal is the genus of a species. For example, 'movement is the genus of sensation' or 'malaka is the genus of memory'. Write down the term claimed as genus and the species.
2
Check the Nature: Malaka vs Fi'l
Determine whether the claimed genus is a malaka (psychic disposition) or a fi'l (act). The author says: sensation is a principle (mabda') of movement, not movement itself. Therefore, movement (an act) cannot be the genus of an act's principle. Similarly, memory is a renewal, while malaka is stability; they are opposites.
A genus must share the same mode: malaka for malaka, fi'l for fi'l.
3
Verify Faculty Compatibility
Check if the genus belongs to the same faculty of the soul as the species. For example, modesty is in the rational faculty (natiqa), while cavity (jawf) is in the animal faculty (hayawaniyya). Anger is in the irascible faculty (ghadabiyya), while pain is in sensory and grief in appetitive. If they are in different faculties, the genus claim is invalid.
The author states: 'the genus is not in that in which the species is'.
4
Apply the Rule
Summarize: a valid genus must match the species in both its category (malaka/fi'l) and its faculty of the soul. If either condition fails, the claimed genus is false.

Source Text

Chapter: On that. Among the topics by which what is posited as a genus is invalidated are: that malaka is made a genus for act, and act is made a genus for malaka, such as one who says that sensation is a bodily movement; movement is an act not a principle of act, while sensation is a principle of act; or that memory is a psychic malaka, and psychic malaka is in a state of stability not in a state of renewal and act, while memory is in a state of renewal not in a state of stability. Then, the genus is not in that in which the species is, rather the species is in that which does not contain the genus, so the genus is not a genus, as one who says that modesty has a cavity, but the cavity is for the animal [soul] from the animal psychic faculty, while modesty is in the rational [faculty]. Also one who says that anger is pain or grief, for anger is in the irascible [faculty], pain in the sensory, and grief in the appetitive or the ruling [faculty]. In summary, among things there are those that give the illusion that they are a genus for something else, but they are not a genus in reality, rather it is impossible, just as when a part of a thing is related to the thing, it gives the illusion that that part is a genus for that thing, as is well-known: that body is a genus for man while it is a part of him. And there is a difference between the body that is a part of man and the body that is the genus of man. How could it not be, since one of them is predicated of man and the other is not, as known in the discussion of predication. Similarly, there is a difference between the body that is part of the constitution and the body that is part of the definition; so the correctness of this topic is known, and it is understood that the body here is not the body that is part of the definition but rather the body that is part of the constitution.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of common errors in assigning genus. The author presents several ways to invalidate false genus claims. First, he gives the case where a disposition (malaka) is mistakenly taken as the genus for its act, or vice versa. For example, sensation is defined as a bodily movement, but movement is an act, not a principle of act, while sensation is a principle. Similarly, memory is a psychic disposition, but disposition implies stability, while memory involves renewal; thus memory cannot be a species under disposition as genus. Second, he notes the condition that the genus cannot be found in the same faculty as the species; the species is in a different faculty. Examples: modesty is in the rational faculty but its supposed genus 'cavity' is in the animal faculty; anger is in the irascible faculty, while its supposed genus 'pain' or 'grief' belongs to sensory or appetitive faculties. Third, he summarizes that some things give the illusion of being genus because they are parts of the thing, like body is a part of man, but it is mistakenly taken as genus. He carefully distinguishes between body as a part of man's constitution and body as a genus in the definition; only the latter is a true genus. All examples are from the Islamic tradition of classifying faculties of the soul.

Avoiding Part-Whole Confusion in Genus Assignment
Author uses part-whole example to illustrate how a part can be mistaken for a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a part of a thing and its genus, using predication and definitional criteria.

Core Idea
A part cannot be the genus of the whole; body as genus differs from body as constituent part.

When assigning a genus, one must be careful not to confuse a part of the thing with its universal genus. The author illustrates this with the body-man example: body can be a part of man (material constitution) or a genus of man (in the definition). The difference lies in how each is predicated and what role it plays in the definition. This lesson trains you to detect such confusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Part and the Whole
Find the claim where a term is said to be a genus, but it is also a part of the thing. Example: 'body is the genus of man' while body is part of man.
2
Check Predication
Apply the test of predication: The genus 'body' (jism) is predicated of man (e.g., 'Man is a body'), but the part-body is not predicated of man in the same way (e.g., 'Man is a body' in the sense of having a body, not being a body).
The author says: 'one of them is predicated of man and the other is not'.
3
Differentiate Definitional Role
The body that is part of the definition (jism al-hadd) is the genus, while the body that is part of the constitution (juz' al-qiwam) is a material component. They are distinct concepts.
4
Apply the Distinction
When evaluating a genus claim, ask: Is the candidate genus truly predicated of the species as a universal? If the candidate is merely a part (material or otherwise), it cannot be a genus.

Mutual Division Prevents Genus-Species Hierarchy
Author shows that if each of two terms can be divided by the other, neither can be genus or species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two terms are mutually dividing and explain why neither can serve as genus or differentia for the other.

Core Idea
If two terms split each other's meaning, neither can be a genus or differentia.

Not every pair of related terms can form a genus-species hierarchy. In this lesson, we examine a critical condition from classical logic: when each term applies to some instances of the other and some outside, they are 'mutually dividing.' The author uses the example of 'animal' and 'white' to show that such terms cannot stand in a genus-differentia relationship. Understanding this rule prevents mistakes in constructing definitions (ḥadd) and descriptions (rasm) across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define mutual division
Check each term: does it apply to some instances of the other AND also to instances outside the other? For 'animal' and 'white': animal applies to some white things (e.g., white horse) and some non-white things (e.g., black horse); white applies to some animals (e.g., white horse) and some non-animals (e.g., snow). So they mutually divide.
This is the key test: find at least one instance where A applies to B, one where A applies to non-B, one where B applies to A, and one where B applies to non-A.
2
Apply the mutual division condition for genus
Recall that a genus must be predicated of the species and differentiate it from other species. If A divides B (A applies to some B and some non-B), then A cannot be the genus of B because a genus must apply to all B. Similarly, if B divides A, B cannot be the genus of A.
Here both divide each other, so neither can be genus of the other.
3
Check the differentia condition
A differentia must apply uniquely to the species (all members of the species and no others). But here, white applies to some animals and some non-animals; so it is not unique to animal. Animal applies to white and non-white; so it is not unique to white. Thus neither is a differentia for the other.
Mutual division destroys the exclusivity required for a differentia.
4
Generalize the rule
Whenever two terms mutually divide, they cannot be arranged in a genus-species hierarchy. This is a universal check for the soundness of any definition involving those terms.
This principle is used in usul al-fiqh to test definitions of legal concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذه المواضع أيضًا ما إذا كان كل واحد من الأمرين ينقسم بالآخر فإنه لا يفهم أن هذا جنس لذلك أو فصله كما في مثل الحيوان والأبيض؛ فإن الحيوان يقع على الأبيض وغير الأبيض، والأبيض يقع على الحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ فلا يكون هذا أولى بالجنسية عن ذلك، ولا بالفصلية كذلك. وقد مرَّ من قبل أن كل واحد منهما لا يصلح لكونه جنسًا ولا لكونه فصلا لصاحبه.
Also among these topics is: when each of the two things is divided by the other, it is not understood that this is a genus for that or a differentia, as in the example of animal and white. For animal applies to white and non-white, and white applies to animal and non-animal. So this is not more deserving of being genus than that, nor more deserving of being differentia. It has already been mentioned before that each of them is not suitable for being a genus nor for being a differentia for the other.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage defines a condition for genus-species relations: two terms that divide each other's extensions cannot be in a genus-species hierarchy. The author gives the example of 'animal' and 'white'. Animal is predicated of some white things (e.g., white horses) and some non-white things; white is predicated of some animals and some non-animals. Thus neither is wholly subordinate to the other; they are cross-cutting. Therefore, neither can be the genus or differentia of the other. This is a fundamental rule in defining terms.

Key Terms

الجنسية

Key Takeaway
Mutual division of two terms eliminates any possibility of one being the genus or differentia of the other, a fundamental rule for sound definitions.

The Error in Defining Wonder: Genus and Differentia Reversed
Author critiques reversal of genus and differentia in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct genus and differentia for 'wonder' (al-tahayyur) and explain why defining it as 'excess of astonishment' is a logical error.

Core Idea
Defining wonder as 'excess of astonishment' mistakes the differentia for the genus.

In this passage, the author warns against a common mistake: thinking that 'excess of astonishment' is a valid definition for wonder. Actually, the correct definition is 'excessive astonishment', where astonishment is the genus and excess is the differentia. Let's see why the reversal is problematic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term being defined is 'wonder' (al-tahayyur). Find it in the Arabic.
2
Extract the incorrect definition
The incorrect definition given is 'excess of astonishment' (ifrat al-ta'ajjub). Write it down.
3
Determine the genus and differentia in the incorrect version
In 'excess of astonishment', the word 'excess' is treated as the genus and 'astonishment' as a differentia. That is a swap.
The correct relation is that astonishment is the genus and excess is the differentia.
4
Contrast with the correct definition
The correct definition is 'excessive astonishment' (ta'ajjub mufrit), where astonishment is the genus and excess is the differentia.
5
Explain the logical contradiction
If wonder were 'excess of astonishment', then wonder would be a property of astonishment, not a species of it. The author shows this leads to absurdity: wonder would then be in astonishment, not identical to it.
Read the explanation in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقريب من هذا إذا لم يكن بين الأمرين إلى الجنسية نسبة واحدة بل أحدهما بعينه جنس، والآخر بعينه فصل لكن قد غلط موضع الذي هو فصل منهما أن يكون جنسًا لما هو جنس منهما كمن يقول إن التحير هو إفراط التعجب ولم يقل معجب مفرط، أو إن التصديق قوة الرأى ولم يقل رأى قوى بجعل الإفراط جنسًا والتعجب فصلا. وكذلك القوة جنسًا والرأي فصلا حتى جعل القوة في الرأي تصديقا لا الرأي الذي فيه قوة؛ فإن الشيء الذي يكون في الشيء لا يكون هو في نفسه فكذلك لم يجعل التحير تعجباً بحال بل حالا في التعجب، وهذا محال فإن الرأي نفسه في المصدق هو التصديق، والتعجب نفسه في المتحير هو التحير . وأما إفراط التعجب فأمر في التعجب؛ فإن كان إفراط التعجب هو التحير نفسه إذا قيل إن هذا ضده فالتحير يكون موجودا في التعجب لأنه إفراط، فيكون التعجب هو التحير لا المتعجب وذلك محال، وكذلك التصديق.
And close to this is when there is not a single relation of genus between the two things, but rather one of them is specifically the genus and the other is specifically the differentia, yet one errs in making the one which is the differentia become a genus for that which is the genus, such as one who says that wonder is excess of astonishment, and does not say 'excessively astonished', or that assent is the strength of opinion, and does not say 'strong opinion', by making excess a genus and astonishment a differentia. Similarly, making strength a genus and opinion a differentia, so that strength in opinion becomes assent, not the opinion which has strength. For a thing that is in a thing does not itself be in itself; thus he did not make wonder an astonishment but rather a state in astonishment. And this is impossible, for the opinion itself in the assenter is assent, and astonishment itself in the amazed is wonder. As for excess of astonishment, it is a matter in astonishment; if excess of astonishment is wonder itself when it is said that this is its opposite, then wonder would exist in astonishment because it is excess, so astonishment would be wonder, not the amazed one, and that is impossible, and likewise for assent.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage critiques a specific type of error in definition: when someone correctly identifies a genus and differentia but reverses them, making the differentia the genus and the genus the differentia. The author gives two examples. First, defining 'wonder' (al-tahayyur) as 'excess of astonishment' (ifrat al-ta'ajjub). Here, the correct definition should be 'excessive astonishment', where 'astonishment' is the genus and 'excess' is the differentia. But by saying 'excess of astonishment', the definer makes 'excess' the genus and 'astonishment' a differentia, which is a logical error. Second, defining 'assent' (al-tasdiq) as 'strength of opinion' (quwwat al-ra'y) instead of 'strong opinion'. The author explains that by making 'strength' a genus and 'opinion' a differentia, one implies that strength in opinion is assent, rather than opinion that has strength. This is impossible because the thing that is in another (e.g., strength in opinion) is not the same as the thing itself (the opinion that has strength). He further explains that if wonder were the same as excess of astonishment, then astonishment would contain wonder, leading to a contradiction. These examples illustrate the importance of correctly placing genus and differentia in definitions.

Key Terms
التحير
التعجب
الإفراط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is wrong with defining 'wonder' as 'excess of astonishment'?
A) It is too vague.
B) It makes excess the genus and astonishment the differentia, whereas astonishment should be the genus.
C) It defines wonder as astonishment, not as excess.
D) It confuses excess with astonishment.

Key Takeaway
The error of reversing genus and differentia leads to definitions that are logically incoherent and must be avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the correct definition for 'assent' (al-tasdiq) using the same pattern: 'strong opinion' rather than 'strength of opinion'. Explain why the latter is incorrect.

The Error in Defining Assent: Strength of Opinion vs. Strong Opinion
Author critiques reversal of genus and differentia in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct genus and differentia for 'assent' (al-tasdiq) and explain why defining it as 'strength of opinion' is a logical error.

Core Idea
Assent is a species of opinion, not a species of strength.

The second example in this passage critiques a definition of assent as 'strength of opinion'. The author shows that the correct definition should be 'strong opinion', with opinion as the genus and strength as the differentia. We will analyze why swapping them leads to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term being defined is 'assent' (al-tasdiq). Find it in the Arabic.
2
Extract the incorrect definition
The incorrect definition given is 'strength of opinion' (quwwat al-ra'y). Write it down.
3
Determine the genus and differentia in the incorrect version
In 'strength of opinion', the word 'strength' is treated as the genus and 'opinion' as a differentia. That is a swap.
The correct relation is that opinion is the genus and strength is the differentia.
4
Contrast with the correct definition
The correct definition is 'strong opinion' (ra'y qawiyy), where opinion is the genus and strength is the differentia.
5
Explain the logical contradiction
If assent were 'strength of opinion', then strength would be the genus and opinion would be a differentia, implying that assent is a kind of strength, not a kind of opinion. The author shows this is impossible: strength in opinion is not the same as opinion that has strength.
Read the explanation in the passage.

Two Terms Dividing Each Other: Genus and Species Impossibility
Author identifies the fallacy when two terms have overlapping extensions and neither is genus for the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two terms divide each other and explain why neither can be the genus or differentia for the other.

Core Idea
When each term applies both inside and outside the other's extension, neither can be genus or differentia.

In defining terms, we often expect a clear genus-species hierarchy. But what happens when two terms overlap without one being fully contained in the other? The author warns that when each term is predicated of both instances of the other and instances outside it, we cannot designate one as the genus or differentia of the other. This principle safeguards definitions from faulty classification.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two terms
Identify the two terms in question and consider their extensions—the set of things they apply to. For example, 'animal' and 'white'.
2
Check if each applies to the other
See if each term is predicated of some individuals to which the other applies, and also of some outside. For 'animal': it applies to white and non-white things. For 'white': it applies to animals and non-animals.
This cross-application shows mutual division.
3
Determine if one is fully within the other
If one term's extension is entirely included in the other's, it could be a species. Here, neither is fully included—each has instances outside the other.
If one were fully inside, the other could be its genus.
4
Apply the rule
Since the terms mutually divide each other, neither can serve as the genus or differentia for the other. This is a general rule to detect defective definitions.
Refer to the example to confirm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذه المواضع أيضًا ما إذا كان كل واحد من الأمرين ينقسم بالآخر فإنه لا يفهم أن هذا جنس لذلك أو فصله كما في مثل الحيوان والأبيض؛ فإن الحيوان يقع على الأبيض وغير الأبيض، والأبيض يقع على الحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ فلا يكون هذا أولى بالجنسية عن ذلك، ولا بالفصلية كذلك. وقد مرَّ من قبل أن كل واحد منهما لا يصلح لكونه جنسًا ولا لكونه فصلا لصاحبه.
And among these topics also is when each of the two things is divided by the other, for it is not understood that this is a genus for that or a differentia, as in the example of animal and white; for animal applies to white and non-white, and white applies to animal and non-animal; so this is not more deserving of being genus than that, nor of being a differentia likewise. And it has passed before that each of them is not suitable for being a genus nor for being a differentia for the other.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage identifies a logical fallacy in definition: when two terms are such that each divides the other (i.e., each is predicated of some instances of the other and also of some instances outside), we cannot assign one as genus and the other as species or differentia. For example, 'animal' and 'white': animal is said of white and non-white things; white is said of animal and non-animal things. Neither is more appropriate to be the genus. The author reminds that this was already discussed: neither can be genus or differentia for the other. This principle is crucial for constructing correct definitions in Islamic logic, as a genus must be predicated of all its species in answer to 'what is it?', and a differentia must differentiate the species from others within the same genus.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
ينقسم بالآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs exemplifies the fallacy where each term divides the other, so neither can be genus of the other?
A) Animal and white
B) Man and animal
C) Body and living
D) Substance and body

Key Takeaway
For two terms to be in a genuine genus-species relation, one must be fully contained within the other; if each divides the other, neither can be genus or differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair 'thing' (shay') and 'existent' (mawjud). In Islamic metaphysics, are they in a genus-species relation? Check if each divides the other using the rule from this lesson.
?
Take the pair 'human' (insan) and 'prophet' (nabi). Can 'human' be the genus for 'prophet'? Apply the rule to decide.

Swapping Genus and Differentia in Relational Definitions
Author illustrates the error of swapping genus and differentia in definitions involving relations like excess and strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct the logical error of swapping genus and differentia in definitions involving relational attributes like excess or strength.

Core Idea
In a definition, the genus must be the substance, not an accidental relation.

This passage from the logic text warns against a subtle mistake: when defining terms like 'bewilderment' or 'assent', one might accidentally make the relational attribute (excess, strength) the genus instead of the differentia. The author shows why this leads to absurdity and how to avoid it. Mastering this distinction is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and proposed definition
Find the term being defined (e.g., bewilderment, assent) and the definition given (e.g., 'excess of wonder', 'strength of opinion').
2
Extract the putative genus and differentia
In the proposed definition, determine which word is intended as the broader category (genus) and which as the distinguishing feature (differentia). For 'excess of wonder', excess is treated as genus and wonder as differentia.
3
Identify the relational attribute
The relational attribute (excess, strength) is an accident, not a substance. It must serve as the differentia, not the genus.
4
Check if the genus is the proper subject
The genus should be the thing that the relational attribute qualifies. For bewilderment, the subject is wonder; for assent, it is opinion. If the relational attribute is made the genus, the definition becomes circular or absurd.
5
Correct the definition
Swap the roles: make the subject the genus and the relational attribute the differentia. For example, say 'excessively wondering' instead of 'excess of wonder'.
The corrected definition avoids the absurdity described in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقريب من هذا إذا لم يكن بين الأمرين إلى الجنسية نسبة واحدة بل أحدهما بعينه جنس، والآخر بعينه فصل لكن قد غلط موضع الذي هو فصل منهما أن يكون جنسًا لما هو جنس منهما كمن يقول إن التحير هو إفراط التعجب ولم يقل معجب مفرط، أو إن التصديق قوة الرأى ولم يقل رأى قوى بجعل الإفراط جنسًا والتعجب فصلا. وكذلك القوة جنسًا والرأي فصلا حتى جعل القوة في الرأي تصديقا لا الرأي الذي فيه قوة؛ فإن الشيء الذي يكون في الشيء لا يكون هو في نفسه فكذلك لم يجعل التحير تعجباً بحال بل حالا في التعجب، وهذا محال فإن الرأي نفسه في المصدق هو التصديق، والتعجب نفسه في المتحير هو التحير . وأما إفراط التعجب فأمر في التعجب؛ فإن كان إفراط التعجب هو التحير نفسه إذا قيل إن هذا ضده فالتحير يكون موجودا في التعجب لأنه إفراط، فيكون التعجب هو التحير لا المتعجب وذلك محال، وكذلك التصديق.
And similar to this is when there is not a single relationship of genus between the two things, but rather one is specifically a genus and the other is specifically a differentia, but the mistake is in the place of that which is the differentia of them, making it a genus for that which is the genus of them, such as someone saying that bewilderment is the excess of wonder, and not saying 'excessively wondering', or that assent is the strength of opinion, and not saying 'strong opinion', by making excess a genus and wonder a differentia. And similarly making strength a genus and opinion a differentia, such that he made the strength in the opinion to be assent, not the opinion which has strength in it. For a thing that is in a thing is not itself in itself. Likewise he did not make bewilderment to be wonder at all, but rather a state in wonder, and this is impossible. For the opinion itself in the one who assents is the assent, and wonder itself in the one who is bewildered is the bewilderment. And as for the excess of wonder, it is a thing in wonder; if the excess of wonder is bewilderment itself, when it is said that this is its opposite, then bewilderment would exist in wonder because it is excess, so wonder would be bewilderment, not the one who wonders, and that is impossible. And similarly for assent.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle error in definition when two terms have a proper genus-differentia relationship, but a person mistakenly swaps them. For example, bewilderment (tahayyur) is actually a state of being excessively wondering, so the proper definition would be 'excessively wondering' (where 'excess' is the differentia and 'wonder' is the genus). But someone might define bewilderment as 'excess of wonder', making 'excess' the genus and 'wonder' the differentia. Similarly, assent (tasdiq) is a strong opinion, but someone might define it as 'strength of opinion'. The error is that the relation (excess, strength) is not the thing itself; it is a state in the subject. The author explains the absurdity: if bewilderment is the excess of wonder, then bewilderment would be in wonder, and wonder would become bewilderment, which is impossible. In Islamic logic, this fallacy undermines correct definitions, as the genus must be the substance or essence, not an accidental relation.

Key Terms
تحير (tahayyur): Bewilderment; defined as a state of excessive wonder, with wonder as genus and excess as differentia. [Note: No significant shift—this term is stable in logical contexts.]
تعجب (ta'ajjub): Wonder; considered a genus for bewilderment when correctly defined. [Note: No significant shift.]
إفراط (ifrat): Excess; a relational attribute that should be the differentia, not the genus, in such definitions. [Note: No significant shift.]
تصديق (tasdiq): Assent; defined as strong (qawi) opinion, with opinion as genus and strength as differentia. [Note: No significant shift in logical usage; in kalam, tasdiq also means belief.]
رأي (ra'y): Opinion; considered a genus for assent when correctly defined. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical error in defining 'assent' as 'strength of opinion'?
A) It makes strength the genus instead of the differentia
B) It makes opinion the differentia instead of the genus
C) It defines assent by something not in its essence
D) It reverses the subject and predicate

Key Takeaway
Correct definition requires that the genus be the subject and the differentia be the relational attribute, not vice versa, otherwise the definition becomes logically absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the logical error in this definition: 'Riba is the excess in a loan.' Then correct it by stating the proper genus and differentia.
?
Analyse the definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) as 'extension of ruling from original to branch due to common cause'. Check whether the differentia correctly distinguishes qiyas from mere analogy.
